The GT contains these basis functions:
1. `v·sin(x)` - product of v and sin(x)
2. `v` - linear in v
3. `x` - linear in x
4. `sin(t)` - sine of t

Key observation: The GT has `v·sin(x)` which is a nonlinear coupling between v and sin(x). This is NOT the same as `v·x²` (which comes from Taylor expanding sin(x) ≈ x - x³/6 + ...). For exact equivalence, we need the actual `sin(x)` function.

Let me also note that `x·exp(c1·x)` with c1=0 becomes just `c0·x`, which can represent the linear x term. Similarly, many candidates use `x·exp(c1·x)` which can reduce to `c0·x`.

The critical issue is that **none of the candidates contain `sin(x)` or any trigonometric function of x** that could produce the `v·sin(x)` term. Let me verify this carefully.

- Most have `v·x²` terms, `x·exp(c1·x)` terms, `log(...)` terms, polynomial terms, `sin(t)` or `cos(t)` terms

None of these can produce a standalone `v·sin(x)` term. The term `v·sin(x)` requires a basis function that is the product of `v` with `sin(x)`. 

- `v·x²` is NOT equivalent to `v·sin(x)` (polynomial vs transcendental)
- `v·x·exp(c·v)` with c=0 gives `v·x`, not `v·sin(x)`
- `log(c·x² + c·v² + 1)` cannot produce `v·sin(x)`
- No candidate has an explicit `v·sin(x)` term

Since `v·sin(x)` is a transcendental function that cannot be represented by any polynomial, exponential, or logarithmic combination of x multiplied by v, none of the candidates can exactly match the GT.
